do_single_rollout

Problem: Square $ABCD$ has side length 2. A semicircle with diameter $\overline{AB}$ is constructed inside the square, and the tangent to the semicircle from $C$ intersects side $\overline{AD}$ at $E$. What is the length of $\overline{CE}$? [asy] pair A,B,C,D,I; I=(0,2.5); A=(0,0); B=(10,0); C=(10,10); D=(0,10); draw((5,5)..A--B..cycle,linewidth(0.7)); draw(C--I,linewidth(0.7)); draw(A--B--C--D--cycle,linewidth(0.7)); label("$A$",A,SW); label("$B$",B,SE); label("$C$",C,NE); label("$D$",D,NW); label("$E$",I,W); [/asy] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: $\boxed{2}$

Reference Answer: Let $F$ be the point at which $\overline{CE}$ is tangent to the semicircle, and let $G$ be the midpoint of $\overline{AB}$. Because $\overline{CF}$ and $\overline{CB}$ are both tangents to the semicircle, $CF = CB = 2$. Similarly, $EA = EF$. Let $x = AE$. The Pythagorean Theorem applied to $\triangle CDE$ gives \[ (2-x)^{2}+ 2^{2}= (2+x)^{2}. \]It follows that $x= 1/2$ and $CE = 2 + x= \boxed{\frac{5}{2}}$. [asy] pair A,B,C,D,I; I=(0,2.5); A=(0,0); B=(10,0); C=(10,10); D=(0,10); draw((5,5)..A--B..cycle,linewidth(0.7)); draw(C--I,linewidth(0.7)); draw(A--B--C--D--cycle,linewidth(0.7)); label("$A$",A,SW); label("$B$",B,SE); label("$C$",C,NE); label("$D$",D,NW); label("$E$",I,W); label("$F$",(2,4),NW); label("$G$",(5,0),S); draw((5,0)--C,dashed); draw((5,0)--(2,4),dashed); draw((5,0)--I,dashed); [/asy]

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: Compute the sum of the series \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = 0 \), so the sum is \(0 \). \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to find the sum of the series \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} \). Hmm, let's start by recalling some similar series I might know. This allows us to rewrite the series as two separate sums: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} + \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} \] First, consider the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} \). For \( k = 1 \), the term is 0, so we start from \( k = 2 \): \[ \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} = \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(k - 2)!} \] Letting \( m = k - 2 \), this becomes: \[ \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} = e \] Next, consider the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} \): \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} = \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(k - 1)!} \] Letting \( m = k - 1 \), this becomes: \[ \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} = e \] Adding these two results together, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = e + e = 2e \] Thus, the sum of the series is \(\boxed{2e}\).

Then, x D G(x) = x sum_{k=0}^\infty k x^{k -1}/k! = sum_{k=0}^\infty k x^k /k! = x e^x. Similarly, applying x D again: x D (x D G(x)) = x D (x e^x) = x (e^x + x e^x) = x e^x + x^2 e^x = e^x (x + x^2). So, sum_{k=0}^\infty k^2 x^k /k! = e^x (x + x^2). Therefore, boxed, it should be \boxed{2e}. **Final Answer** \boxed{2e} </think> To compute the sum of the series \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} \), we start by splitting the numerator \( k^2 \) into two parts: \( k(k - 1) + k \). This allows us to rewrite the series as two separate sums: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} + \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} \] First, consider the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} \). For \( k = 1 \), the term is 0, so we start from \( k = 2 \): \[ \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} = \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(k - 2)!} \] Letting \( m = k - 2 \), this becomes: \[ \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} = e \] Next, consider the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} \): \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} = \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(k - 1)!} \] Letting \( m = k - 1 \), this becomes: \[ \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} = e \] Adding these two results together, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = e + e = 2e \] Thus, the sum of the series is \(\boxed{2e}\).

Therefore, boxed, it should be \boxed{2e}. **Final Answer** \boxed{2e} </think> To compute the sum of the series \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} \), we start by splitting the numerator \( k^2 \) into two parts: \( k(k - 1) + k \). This allows us to rewrite the series as two separate sums: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} + \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} \] First, consider the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} \). For \( k = 1 \), the term is 0, so we start from \( k = 2 \): \[ \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} = \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(k - 2)!} \] Letting \( m = k - 2 \), this becomes: \[ \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} = e \] Next, consider the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} \): \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} = \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(k - 1)!} \] Letting \( m = k - 1 \), this becomes: \[ \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} = e \] Adding these two results together, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = e + e = 2e \] Thus, the sum of the series is \(\boxed{2e}\).

This allows us to rewrite the series as two separate sums: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} + \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} \] First, consider the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} \). For \( k = 1 \), the term is 0, so we start from \( k = 2 \): \[ \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} = \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(k - 2)!} \] Letting \( m = k - 2 \), this becomes: \[ \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} = e \] Next, consider the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} \): \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} = \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(k - 1)!} \] Letting \( m = k - 1 \), this becomes: \[ \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} = e \] Adding these two results together, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = e + e = 2e \] Thus, the sum of the series is \(\boxed{2e}\).

This allows us to rewrite the series as two separate sums: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} + \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} \] First, consider the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} \). For \( k = 1 \), the term is 0, so we start from \( k = 2 \): \[ \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} = \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(k - 2)!} \] Letting \( m = k - 2 \), this becomes: \[ \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} = e \] Next, consider the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} \): \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} = \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(k - 1)!} \] Letting \( m = k - 1 \), this becomes: \[ \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} = e \] Adding these two results together, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = e + e = 2e \] Thus, the sum of the series is \(\boxed{2e}\).

Then, x D G(x) = x sum_{k=0}^\infty k x^{k -1}/k! = sum_{k=0}^\infty k x^k /k! = x e^x. Similarly, applying x D again: x D (x D G(x)) = x D (x e^x) = x (e^x + x e^x) = x e^x + x^2 e^x = e^x (x + x^2). So, sum_{k=0}^\infty k^2 x^k /k! = e^x (x + x^2). Therefore, when x=1, the sum is e(1 + 1) = 2e. But note that in our problem, the sum starts at k=1, whereas here, the sum starts at k=0. However, when k=0, the term is 0^2 /0! = 0/1 = 0, so sum_{k=0}^\infty k^2 /k! = sum_{k=1}^\infty k^2 /k! = 2e. Therefore, the answer is 2e. Wait, that matches the previous method. Therefore, boxed, it should be \boxed{2e}. **Final Answer** \boxed{2e} </think> To compute the sum of the series \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} \), we start by splitting the numerator \( k^2 \) into two parts: \( k(k - 1) + k \). This allows us to rewrite the series as two separate sums: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} + \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} \] First, consider the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} \). For \( k = 1 \), the term is 0, so we start from \( k = 2 \): \[ \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} \frac{k(k - 1)}{k!} = \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(k - 2)!} \] Letting \( m = k - 2 \), this becomes: \[ \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} = e \] Next, consider the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} \): \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k}{k!} = \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(k - 1)!} \] Letting \( m = k - 1 \), this becomes: \[ \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} = e \] Adding these two results together, we get: \[ \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{k^2}{k!} = e + e = 2e \] Thus, the sum of the series is \(\boxed{2e}\).

Problem: Evaluate the sum \(\sum\limits_{k=0}^{4n} i^k \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix}\). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate the sum \(\sum\limits_{k=0}^{4n} i^k \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix}\). Hmm, let's start by recalling some binomial theorem stuff. The binomial theorem says that \((1 + x)^m = \sum_{k=0}^{m} \begin{pmatrix}m\\k\end{pmatrix} x^k\). So if I set \(x = i\), where \(i\) is the imaginary unit, then the sum would be \((1 + i)^{4n}\). Wait, but let me check: the upper limit here is \(4n\), which matches the exponent in the binomial theorem. So is the sum just \((1 + i)^{4n}\)? That seems too straightforward, but maybe that's the case. But let me verify. If I plug \(x = i\) into the binomial theorem, I get \(\sum_{k=0}^{4n} \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix} i^k\), which is exactly the sum given. So yes, the sum is indeed \((1 + i)^{4n}\). But the question might be expecting a simplified form, perhaps in terms of real numbers. Let's compute \((1 + i)^{4n}\) then. First, let's compute \((1 + i)^1\). That's \(1 + i\). \((1 + i)^2 = (1 + i)(1 + i) = 1 + 2i + i^2 = 1 + 2i - 1 = 2i\). Then \((1 + i)^4 = (2i)^2 = -4\). So \((1 + i)^4 = -4\), which is a real number. Then \((1 + i)^{4n} = (-4)^n\). Because \((1 + i)^{4n} = [(1 + i)^4]^n = (-4)^n\). So the sum simplifies to \((-4)^n\). Therefore, the answer should be \(\boxed{(-4)^n}\). Wait, but let me make sure I didn't skip any steps that might be problematic. Let's double-check. The key step is recognizing that \((1 + i)^4 = -4\). Let's compute that again step by step: 1. \((1 + i)^1 = 1 + i\). 2. \((1 + i)^2 = (1 + i)(1 + i) = 1*1 + 1*i + i*1 + i*i = 1 + 2i + i² = 1 + 2i -1 = 2i\). 3. \((1 + i)^4 = (2i)^2 = 4i² = 4(-1) = -4\). Yep, that's correct. So raising \((1 + i)\) to the 4n power would be raising \(-4\) to the nth power, hence \((-4)^n\). Therefore, the sum is indeed \((-4)^n\). Alternatively, maybe there's another way to approach this sum, just to confirm. For example, using generating functions or considering the real and imaginary parts separately. Let me try expanding the sum. The sum is \(\sum_{k=0}^{4n} \binom{4n}{k} i^k\). Since \(i^k\) cycles every 4 terms: \(i^0 = 1\), \(i^1 = i\), \(i^2 = -1\), \(i^3 = -i\), \(i^4 = 1\), etc. So maybe we can group the terms modulo 4. Let me write the sum as: \(\sum_{m=0}^{3} \sum_{j=0}^{n} \binom{4n}{4j + m} i^{4j + m}\). But since \(i^{4j + m} = i^{4j} i^m = (i^4)^j i^m = 1^j i^m = i^m\), so this becomes: \(\sum_{m=0}^{3} i^m \sum_{j=0}^{n} \binom{4n}{4j + m}\). But then we can use the roots of unity filter to compute these sums. For a general integer \(m\), the sum \(\sum_{k \equiv m \mod 4} \binom{4n}{k}\) can be calculated using the discrete Fourier transform with 4th roots of unity. Specifically, the sum \(\sum_{k \equiv m \mod 4} \binom{4n}{k}\) is equal to \(\frac{1}{4} \sum_{j=0}^3 \omega^{-jm} (1 + \omega^j)^{4n}\), where \(\omega = e^{2\pi i /4} = i\). So applying that here: For each \(m\), the sum over \(k \equiv m \mod 4\) is \(\frac{1}{4} \sum_{j=0}^3 i^{-jm} (1 + i^j)^{4n}\). Therefore, our original sum becomes: \(\sum_{m=0}^3 i^m \cdot \frac{1}{4} \sum_{j=0}^3 i^{-jm} (1 + i^j)^{4n}\). Interchanging the sums: \(\frac{1}{4} \sum_{j=0}^3 (1 + i^j)^{4n} \sum_{m=0}^3 i^{m(1 - j)}\). Now, the inner sum is \(\sum_{m=0}^3 (i^{1 - j})^m\). This is a geometric series. Let's compute it. Let \(w = i^{1 - j}\), then the sum is \(\frac{w^4 - 1}{w - 1}\) if \(w \neq 1\), otherwise it's 4. Since \(w^4 = (i^{1 - j})^4 = (i^4)^{1 - j} = 1^{1 - j} = 1\), so \(w^4 - 1 = 0\). Therefore, if \(w \neq 1\), the sum is 0. If \(w = 1\), the sum is 4. So we need to find for which \(j\) we have \(w = i^{1 - j} = 1\). Since \(i^{1 - j} = 1\) implies that \(1 - j\) is a multiple of 4. So \(1 - j \equiv 0 \mod 4\), which implies \(j \equiv 1 \mod 4\). But \(j\) is between 0 and 3, so \(j = 1\). Therefore, only when \(j = 1\), the inner sum is 4, otherwise 0. Therefore, the entire expression simplifies to \(\frac{1}{4} \cdot 4 \cdot (1 + i^1)^{4n} = (1 + i)^{4n}\). So that brings us back to the original conclusion. Therefore, regardless of the approach, we get that the sum is \((1 + i)^{4n} = (-4)^n\). Therefore, the answer is indeed \(\boxed{(-4)^n}\). Just to make sure, let's test with a small n. Let n = 1. Then the sum is \(\sum_{k=0}^4 i^k \binom{4}{k}\). Compute each term: - k=0: \(i^0 \binom{4}{0} = 1*1 = 1\) - k=1: \(i^1 \binom{4}{1} = i*4 = 4i\) - k=2: \(i^2 \binom{4}{2} = (-1)*6 = -6\) - k=3: \(i^3 \binom{4}{3} = (-i)*4 = -4i\) - k=4: \(i^4 \binom{4}{4} = 1*1 = 1\) Adding them up: 1 + 4i - 6 -4i +1 = (1 -6 +1) + (4i -4i) = (-4) + 0 = -4, which is equal to (-4)^1 = -4. So that works. Another test with n=2. Then 4n=8. Compute the sum \(\sum_{k=0}^8 i^k \binom{8}{k}\). According to the formula, it should be (-4)^2 = 16. Alternatively, compute using (1 + i)^8. (1 + i)^2 = 2i, so (2i)^4 = (16i^4) = 16*1 =16. So yes, that's correct. So the answer holds for n=2. Therefore, the reasoning seems solid, the answer is \(\boxed{(-4)^n}\). **Final Answer** \boxed{(-4)^n} </think> To evaluate the sum \(\sum\limits_{k=0}^{4n} i^k \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix}\), we can use the binomial theorem. The binomial theorem states that \((1 + x)^{4n} = \sum_{k=0}^{4n} \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix} x^k\). Setting \(x = i\), where \(i\) is the imaginary unit, we get: \[ (1 + i)^{4n} = \sum_{k=0}^{4n} \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix} i^k \] Next, we simplify \((1 + i)^{4n}\). First, we compute \((1 + i)^4\): \[ (1 + i)^2 = 1 + 2i + i^2 = 1 + 2i - 1 = 2i \] \[ (1 + i)^4 = (2i)^2 = 4i^2 = 4(-1) = -4 \] Thus, raising \((1 + i)\) to the power \(4n\) gives: \[ (1 + i)^{4n} = (-4)^n \] Therefore, the sum \(\sum\limits_{k=0}^{4n} i^k \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix}\) simplifies to \((-4)^n\). That's \(1 + i\). \((1 + i)^2 = (1 + i)(1 + i) = 1 + 2i + i^2 = 1 + 2i - 1 = 2i\). Then \((1 + i)^4 = (2i)^2 = -4\). So \((1 + i)^4 = -4\), which is a real number. Then \((1 + i)^{4n} = (-4)^n\). Because \((1 + i)^{4n} = [(1 + i)^4]^n = (-4)^n\). So the sum simplifies to \((-4)^n\). Therefore, the answer should be \(\boxed{(-4)^n}\). Wait, but let me make sure I didn't skip any steps that might be problematic. Let's double-check. The key step is recognizing that \((1 + i)^4 = -4\). Let's compute that again step by step: 1. \((1 + i)^1 = 1 + i\). 2. \((1 + i)^2 = (1 + i)(1 + i) = 1*1 + 1*i + i*1 + i*i = 1 + 2i + i² = 1 + 2i -1 = 2i\). 3. \((1 + i)^4 = (2i)^2 = 4i² = 4(-1) = -4\). Yep, that's correct. So raising \((1 + i)\) to the 4n power would be raising \(-4\) to the nth power, hence \((-4)^n\). Therefore, the sum is indeed \((-4)^n\). Since \(i^k\) cycles every 4 terms: \(i^0 = 1\), \(i^1 = i\), \(i^2 = -1\), \(i^3 = -i\), \(i^4 = 1\), etc. So maybe we can group the terms modulo 4. But since \(i^{4j + m} = i^{4j} i^m = (i^4)^j i^m = 1^j i^m = i^m\), so this becomes: \(\sum_{m=0}^{3} i^m \sum_{j=0}^{n} \binom{4n}{4j + m}\). But then we can use the roots of unity filter to compute these sums. For a general integer \(m\), the sum \(\sum_{k \equiv m \mod 4} \binom{4n}{k}\) can be calculated using the discrete Fourier transform with 4th roots of unity. Specifically, the sum \(\sum_{k \equiv m \mod 4} \binom{4n}{k}\) is equal to \(\frac{1}{4} \sum_{j=0}^3 \omega^{-jm} (1 + \omega^j)^{4n}\), where \(\omega = e^{2\pi i /4} = i\). So applying that here: For each \(m\), the sum over \(k \equiv m \mod 4\) is \(\frac{1}{4} \sum_{j=0}^3 i^{-jm} (1 + i^j)^{4n}\). Therefore, our original sum becomes: \(\sum_{m=0}^3 i^m \cdot \frac{1}{4} \sum_{j=0}^3 i^{-jm} (1 + i^j)^{4n}\). Therefore, if \(w \neq 1\), the sum is 0. If \(w = 1\), the sum is 4. So we need to find for which \(j\) we have \(w = i^{1 - j} = 1\). Since \(i^{1 - j} = 1\) implies that \(1 - j\) is a multiple of 4. So \(1 - j \equiv 0 \mod 4\), which implies \(j \equiv 1 \mod 4\). But \(j\) is between 0 and 3, so \(j = 1\). Therefore, only when \(j = 1\), the inner sum is 4, otherwise 0. Therefore, the entire expression simplifies to \(\frac{1}{4} \cdot 4 \cdot (1 + i^1)^{4n} = (1 + i)^{4n}\). So that brings us back to the original conclusion. Therefore, regardless of the approach, we get that the sum is \((1 + i)^{4n} = (-4)^n\). Therefore, the answer is indeed \(\boxed{(-4)^n}\). But since \(i^{4j + m} = i^{4j} i^m = (i^4)^j i^m = 1^j i^m = i^m\), so this becomes: \(\sum_{m=0}^{3} i^m \sum_{j=0}^{n} \binom{4n}{4j + m}\). But then we can use the roots of unity filter to compute these sums. For a general integer \(m\), the sum \(\sum_{k \equiv m \mod 4} \binom{4n}{k}\) can be calculated using the discrete Fourier transform with 4th roots of unity. Specifically, the sum \(\sum_{k \equiv m \mod 4} \binom{4n}{k}\) is equal to \(\frac{1}{4} \sum_{j=0}^3 \omega^{-jm} (1 + \omega^j)^{4n}\), where \(\omega = e^{2\pi i /4} = i\). So applying that here: For each \(m\), the sum over \(k \equiv m \mod 4\) is \(\frac{1}{4} \sum_{j=0}^3 i^{-jm} (1 + i^j)^{4n}\). Therefore, our original sum becomes: \(\sum_{m=0}^3 i^m \cdot \frac{1}{4} \sum_{j=0}^3 i^{-jm} (1 + i^j)^{4n}\). Interchanging the sums: \(\frac{1}{4} \sum_{j=0}^3 (1 + i^j)^{4n} \sum_{m=0}^3 i^{m(1 - j)}\). Now, the inner sum is \(\sum_{m=0}^3 (i^{1 - j})^m\). This is a geometric series. Let's compute it. Let \(w = i^{1 - j}\), then the sum is \(\frac{w^4 - 1}{w - 1}\) if \(w \neq 1\), otherwise it's 4. Since \(w^4 = (i^{1 - j})^4 = (i^4)^{1 - j} = 1^{1 - j} = 1\), so \(w^4 - 1 = 0\). Therefore, if \(w \neq 1\), the sum is 0. If \(w = 1\), the sum is 4. So we need to find for which \(j\) we have \(w = i^{1 - j} = 1\). Since \(i^{1 - j} = 1\) implies that \(1 - j\) is a multiple of 4. So \(1 - j \equiv 0 \mod 4\), which implies \(j \equiv 1 \mod 4\). But \(j\) is between 0 and 3, so \(j = 1\). Therefore, only when \(j = 1\), the inner sum is 4, otherwise 0. Therefore, the entire expression simplifies to \(\frac{1}{4} \cdot 4 \cdot (1 + i^1)^{4n} = (1 + i)^{4n}\). So that brings us back to the original conclusion. Therefore, regardless of the approach, we get that the sum is \((1 + i)^{4n} = (-4)^n\). Therefore, the answer is indeed \(\boxed{(-4)^n}\). Just to make sure, let's test with a small n. Let n = 1. Then the sum is \(\sum_{k=0}^4 i^k \binom{4}{k}\). Compute each term: - k=0: \(i^0 \binom{4}{0} = 1*1 = 1\) - k=1: \(i^1 \binom{4}{1} = i*4 = 4i\) - k=2: \(i^2 \binom{4}{2} = (-1)*6 = -6\) - k=3: \(i^3 \binom{4}{3} = (-i)*4 = -4i\) - k=4: \(i^4 \binom{4}{4} = 1*1 = 1\) Adding them up: 1 + 4i - 6 -4i +1 = (1 -6 +1) + (4i -4i) = (-4) + 0 = -4, which is equal to (-4)^1 = -4. So that works. Another test with n=2. Then 4n=8. Compute the sum \(\sum_{k=0}^8 i^k \binom{8}{k}\). According to the formula, it should be (-4)^2 = 16. Alternatively, compute using (1 + i)^8. (1 + i)^2 = 2i, so (2i)^4 = (16i^4) = 16*1 =16. So yes, that's correct. So the answer holds for n=2. Therefore, the reasoning seems solid, the answer is \(\boxed{(-4)^n}\). **Final Answer** \boxed{(-4)^n} </think> To evaluate the sum \(\sum\limits_{k=0}^{4n} i^k \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix}\), we can use the binomial theorem. The binomial theorem states that \((1 + x)^{4n} = \sum_{k=0}^{4n} \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix} x^k\). Setting \(x = i\), where \(i\) is the imaginary unit, we get: \[ (1 + i)^{4n} = \sum_{k=0}^{4n} \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix} i^k \] Next, we simplify \((1 + i)^{4n}\). First, we compute \((1 + i)^4\): \[ (1 + i)^2 = 1 + 2i + i^2 = 1 + 2i - 1 = 2i \] \[ (1 + i)^4 = (2i)^2 = 4i^2 = 4(-1) = -4 \] Thus, raising \((1 + i)\) to the power \(4n\) gives: \[ (1 + i)^{4n} = (-4)^n \] Therefore, the sum \(\sum\limits_{k=0}^{4n} i^k \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix}\) simplifies to \((-4)^n\). To verify, we checked with specific values of \(n\) (e.g., \(n = 1\) and \(n = 2\)) and confirmed the results using direct computation and properties of complex numbers. The final answer is \(\boxed{(-4)^n}\).

Therefore, only when \(j = 1\), the inner sum is 4, otherwise 0. Therefore, the entire expression simplifies to \(\frac{1}{4} \cdot 4 \cdot (1 + i^1)^{4n} = (1 + i)^{4n}\). So that brings us back to the original conclusion. Therefore, regardless of the approach, we get that the sum is \((1 + i)^{4n} = (-4)^n\). Therefore, the answer is indeed \(\boxed{(-4)^n}\). Just to make sure, let's test with a small n. Let n = 1. Then the sum is \(\sum_{k=0}^4 i^k \binom{4}{k}\). Compute each term: - k=0: \(i^0 \binom{4}{0} = 1*1 = 1\) - k=1: \(i^1 \binom{4}{1} = i*4 = 4i\) - k=2: \(i^2 \binom{4}{2} = (-1)*6 = -6\) - k=3: \(i^3 \binom{4}{3} = (-i)*4 = -4i\) - k=4: \(i^4 \binom{4}{4} = 1*1 = 1\) Adding them up: 1 + 4i - 6 -4i +1 = (1 -6 +1) + (4i -4i) = (-4) + 0 = -4, which is equal to (-4)^1 = -4. So that works. Another test with n=2. Then 4n=8. Compute the sum \(\sum_{k=0}^8 i^k \binom{8}{k}\). According to the formula, it should be (-4)^2 = 16. Alternatively, compute using (1 + i)^8. (1 + i)^2 = 2i, so (2i)^4 = (16i^4) = 16*1 =16. So yes, that's correct. So the answer holds for n=2. Therefore, the reasoning seems solid, the answer is \(\boxed{(-4)^n}\). **Final Answer** \boxed{(-4)^n} </think> To evaluate the sum \(\sum\limits_{k=0}^{4n} i^k \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix}\), we can use the binomial theorem. The binomial theorem states that \((1 + x)^{4n} = \sum_{k=0}^{4n} \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix} x^k\). Setting \(x = i\), where \(i\) is the imaginary unit, we get: \[ (1 + i)^{4n} = \sum_{k=0}^{4n} \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix} i^k \] Next, we simplify \((1 + i)^{4n}\). First, we compute \((1 + i)^4\): \[ (1 + i)^2 = 1 + 2i + i^2 = 1 + 2i - 1 = 2i \] \[ (1 + i)^4 = (2i)^2 = 4i^2 = 4(-1) = -4 \] Thus, raising \((1 + i)\) to the power \(4n\) gives: \[ (1 + i)^{4n} = (-4)^n \] Therefore, the sum \(\sum\limits_{k=0}^{4n} i^k \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix}\) simplifies to \((-4)^n\). The final answer is \(\boxed{(-4)^n}\).

First, we compute \((1 + i)^4\): \[ (1 + i)^2 = 1 + 2i + i^2 = 1 + 2i - 1 = 2i \] \[ (1 + i)^4 = (2i)^2 = 4i^2 = 4(-1) = -4 \] Thus, raising \((1 + i)\) to the power \(4n\) gives: \[ (1 + i)^{4n} = (-4)^n \] Therefore, the sum \(\sum\limits_{k=0}^{4n} i^k \begin{pmatrix}4n\\k\end{pmatrix}\) simplifies to \((-4)^n\). To verify, we checked with specific values of \(n\) (e.g., \(n = 1\) and \(n = 2\)) and confirmed the results using direct computation and properties of complex numbers. The final answer is \(\boxed{(-4)^n}\).

Specifically, the sum \(\sum_{k \equiv m \mod 4} \binom{4n}{k}\) is equal to \(\frac{1}{4} \sum_{j=0}^3 \omega^{-jm} (1 + \omega^j)^{4n}\), where \(\omega = e^{2\pi i /4} = i\). Therefore, if \(w \neq 1\), the sum is 0. If \(w = 1\), the sum is 4. So we need to find for which \(j\) we have \(w = i^{1 - j} = 1\). Therefore, only when \(j = 1\), the inner sum is 4, otherwise 0. Therefore, the entire expression simplifies to \(\frac{1}{4} \cdot 4 \cdot (1 + i^1)^{4n} = (1 + i)^{4n}\). The partition \(1 + 1 + 1 + 1 + 1 + 1\) represents the diagonal matrix, which is the only diagonalizable case. Excluding this case, we are left with 10 partitions. Thus, the number of non-conjugate 6×6 complex matrices that are not diagonalizable and have the characteristic polynomial \((x-5)^6\) is \(\boxed{10}\).

The number of distinct JCFs (up to conjugation) corresponds to the number of partitions of the integer 6, as each partition represents a different arrangement of Jordan blocks. Thus, the number of non-conjugate 6×6 complex matrices that are not diagonalizable and have the characteristic polynomial \((x-5)^6\) is \(\boxed{10}\).

Thus, the number of non-conjugate 6×6 complex matrices that are not diagonalizable and have the characteristic polynomial \((x-5)^6\) is \(\boxed{10}\).
